Gnosticism">Gnosticism</a></div></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;border:none;">\n<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Socrates.png"><img alt="Socrates.png" class="noviewer" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="326" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/10px-Socrates.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/15px-Socrates.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/21px-Socrates.png 2x" width="10"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Philosophy" title="Portal:Philosophy">Philosophy portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Neoplatonism" title="Template:Neoplatonism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Neoplatonism" title="Template talk:Neoplatonism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Neoplatonism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Giordano Bruno</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/d&#658;/: \'j\' in \'jam\'">d&#658;</span><span title="/&#596;&#720;r/: \'ar\' in \'war\'">&#596;&#720;r</span><span title="/&#712;/: primary stress follows">&#712;</span><span title="\'d\' in \'dye\'">d</span><span title="/&#593;&#720;/: \'a\' in \'father\'">&#593;&#720;</span><span title="\'n\' in \'nigh\'">n</span><span title="/o&#650;/: \'o\' in \'code\'">o&#650;</span></span> <wbr/><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/&#712;/: primary stress follows">&#712;</span><span title="\'b\' in \'buy\'">b</span><span title="\'r\' in \'rye\'">r</span><span title="/u&#720;/: \'oo\' in \'goose\'">u&#720;</span><span title="\'n\' in \'nigh\'">n</span><span title="/o&#650;/: \'o\' in \'code\'">o&#650;</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <small>Italian:&#160;</small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Italian" title="Help:IPA/Italian">[d&#658;or&#712;da&#720;no &#712;bru&#720;no]</a></span>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Iordanus Brunus Nolanus</i>; born <b>Filippo Bruno</b>, 1548 &#8211; 17 February 1600) was an Italian <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominican</a> <a href="/wiki/Friar" title="Friar">friar</a>, philosopher, mathematician, poet, and cosmological theorist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> He is known for his cosmological theories, which conceptually extended the then-novel <a href="/wiki/Copernican_heliocentrism" title="Copernican heliocentrism">Copernican model</a>. He proposed that the stars were distant suns surrounded by their own <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exoplanets" title="Exoplanets">planets</a>, and he raised the possibility that these planets might foster life of their own, a philosophical position known as <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_pluralism" title="Cosmic pluralism">cosmic pluralism</a>. He also insisted that the universe is <a href="/wiki/Infinity" title="Infinity">infinite</a> and could have no "center".\n</p><p>Starting in 1593, Bruno was tried for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_heresy" title="Christian heresy">heresy</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Inquisition" title="Roman Inquisition">Roman Inquisition</a> on charges of denial of several core Catholic doctrines, including <a href="/wiki/Hell" title="Hell">eternal damnation</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">divinity of Christ</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mary_(mother_of_Jesus)" title="Mary (mother of Jesus)">virginity of Mary</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Transubstantiation" title="Transubstantiation">transubstantiation</a>. Bruno\'s <a href="/wiki/Pantheism" title="Pantheism">pantheism</a> was also a matter of grave concern,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> as was his teaching of the <a href="/wiki/Metempsychosis" title="Metempsychosis">transmigration of the soul</a>. The Inquisition found him guilty, and he was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Execution_by_burning" title="Execution by burning">burned at the stake</a> in Rome\'s <a href="/wiki/Campo_de%27_Fiori" title="Campo de\' Fiori">Campo de\' Fiori</a> in 1600. After his death, he gained considerable fame, being particularly celebrated by 19th- and early 20th-century commentators who regarded him as <a href="/wiki/Conflict_thesis" title="Conflict thesis">a martyr for science</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> although historians have debated the extent to which his heresy trial was a response to his astronomical views or to other aspects of his philosophy and theology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>\nBruno\'s case is still considered a landmark in the history of <a href="/wiki/Freethought" title="Freethought">free thought</a> and the emerging sciences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gatti18_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-gatti18-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-montano1_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-montano1-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-birx_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-birx-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In addition to cosmology, Bruno also wrote extensively on the <a href="/wiki/Art_of_memory" title="Art of memory">art of memory</a>, a loosely organized group of <a href="/wiki/Mnemonic" title="Mnemonic">mnemonic</a> techniques and principles. Historian <a href="/wiki/Frances_Yates" title="Frances Yates">Frances Yates</a> argues that Bruno was deeply influenced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab_astrology" title="Arab astrology">Arab astrology</a> (particularly the philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica.com_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannica.com-14">[14]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonism</a>, Renaissance <a href="/wiki/Hermeticism" title="Hermeticism">Hermeticism</a>, and Genesis-like legends surrounding the Egyptian god <a href="/wiki/Thoth" title="Thoth">Thoth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Other studies of Bruno have focused on his qualitative approach to mathematics and his application of the spatial concepts of geometry to language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Life"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Life</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#Early_years,_1548&#8211;1576"><span class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Early years, 1548&#8211;1576</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#First_years_of_wandering,_1576&#8211;1583"><span class="tocnumber">1.2</span> <span class="toctext">First years of wandering, 1576&#8211;1583</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#England,_1583&#8211;1585"><span class="tocnumber">1.3</span> <span class="toctext">England, 1583&#8211;1585</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#Last_years_of_wandering,_1585&#8211;1592"><span class="tocnumber">1.4</span> <span class="toctext">Last years of wandering, 1585&#8211;1592</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Imprisonment,_trial_and_execution,_1593&#8211;1600"><span class="tocnumber">1.5</span> <span class="toctext">Imprisonment, trial and execution, 1593&#8211;1600</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#Physical_appearance"><span class="tocnumber">1.6</span> <span class="toctext">Physical appearance</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-8"><a href="#Cosmology"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Cosmology</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#Contemporary_cosmological_beliefs"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Contemporary cosmological beliefs</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Bruno\'s_cosmological_claims"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Bruno\'s cosmological claims</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#Retrospective_views_of_Bruno"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Retrospective views of Bruno</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Late_Vatican_position"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Late Vatican position</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#A_martyr_of_science"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">A martyr of science</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Theological_heresy"><span class="tocnumber">3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Theological heresy</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#Artistic_depictions"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Artistic depictions</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#References_in_poetry"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">References in poetry</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#Appearances_in_fiction"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Appearances in fiction</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Giordano_Bruno_Foundation"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Giordano Bruno Foundation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Giordano_Bruno_Memorial_Award"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Giordano Bruno Memorial Award</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Astronomical_objects_named_after_Bruno"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Astronomical objects named after Bruno</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Other_remembrances"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Other remembrances</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Works"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Works</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Collections"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Collections</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Giordano_Bruno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="Early_years.2C_1548.E2.80.931576"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years,_1548&#8211;1576">Early years, 1548&#8211;1576</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Giordano_Bruno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early years, 1548&#8211;1576">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Born Filippo  Bruno in <a href="/wiki/Nola" title="Nola">Nola</a> (in <a href="/wiki/Campania" title="Campania">Campania</a>, then part of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sicily" title="Kingdom of Sicily">Kingdom of Sicily</a>, known as the Kingdom of Naples) in 1548, he was the son of Giovanni Bruno, a soldier, and Fraulissa Savolino. In his youth he was sent to <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Naples</a> to be educated. He was tutored privately at the Augustinian monastery there, and attended public lectures at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Studium_Generale" title="Studium Generale">Studium Generale</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> At the age of 17, he entered the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominican Order</a> at the monastery of <a href="/wiki/San_Domenico_Maggiore" title="San Domenico Maggiore">San Domenico Maggiore</a> in <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Naples</a>, taking the name Giordano, after Giordano Crispo, his metaphysics tutor. He continued his studies there, completing his <a href="/wiki/Novitiate" title="Novitiate">novitiate</a>, and became an ordained priest in 1572 at age 24. During his time in Naples he became known for his skill with the art of memory and on one occasion traveled to Rome to demonstrate his mnemonic system before <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_V" title="Pope Pius V">Pope Pius V</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scipione_Rebiba" title="Scipione Rebiba">Cardinal Rebiba</a>. In his later years Bruno claimed that the Pope accepted his dedication to him of the lost work <i>On The Ark of Noah</i> at this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While Bruno was distinguished for outstanding ability, his taste for free thinking and forbidden books soon caused him difficulties. Given the controversy he caused in later life it is surprising that he was able to remain within the monastic system for eleven years. In his testimony to Venetian inquisitors during his trial, many years later, he says that proceedings were twice taken against him for having cast away images of the saints, retaining only a crucifix, and for having recommended controversial texts to a novice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Such behavior could perhaps be overlooked, but Bruno\'s situation became much more serious when he was reported to have defended the <a href="/wiki/Arianism" title="Arianism">Arian heresy</a>, and when a copy of the banned writings of <a href="/wiki/Erasmus" title="Erasmus">Erasmus</a>, annotated by him, was discovered hidden in the convent privy. When he learned that an indictment was being prepared against him in Naples he fled, shedding his <a href="/wiki/Religious_habit" title="Religious habit">religious habit</a>, at least for a time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="First_years_of_wandering.2C_1576.E2.80.931583"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="First_years_of_wandering,_1576&#8211;1583">First years of wandering, 1576&#8211;1583</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Giordano_Bruno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: First years of wandering, 1576&#8211;1583">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Bruno first went to the Genoese port of <a href="/wiki/Noli" title="Noli">Noli</a>, then to <a href="/wiki/Savona" title="Savona">Savona</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turin" title="Turin">Turin</a> and finally to <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>, where he published his lost work <i>On the Signs of the Times</i> with the permission (so he claimed at his trial) of the Dominican Remigio Nannini Fiorentino. From Venice he went to <a href="/wiki/Padua" title="Padua">Padua</a>, where he met fellow Dominicans who convinced him to wear his <a href="/wiki/Religious_habit" title="Religious habit">religious habit</a> again. From Padua he went to <a href="/wiki/Bergamo" title="Bergamo">Bergamo</a> and then across the Alps to <a href="/wiki/Chamb%C3%A9ry" title="Chamb&#233;ry">Chamb&#233;ry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lyon" title="Lyon">Lyon</a>. His movements after this time are obscure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Earlierbruno.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="503" data-file-width="383" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Earlierbruno.jpg/170px-Earlierbruno.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Earlierbruno.jpg/255px-Earlierbruno.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Earlierbruno.jpg/340px-Earlierbruno.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Earlierbruno.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The earliest depiction of Bruno is an engraving published in 1715 in Germany, presumed based on a lost contemporary portrait.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-22">[22]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1579 he arrived in <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a>. As D.W. Singer, a Bruno biographer, notes, "The question has sometimes been raised as to whether Bruno became a Protestant, but it is intrinsically most unlikely that he accepted membership in Calvin\'s communion"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> During his Venetian trial he told inquisitors that while in Geneva he told the Marchese de Vico of Naples, who was notable for helping Italian refugees in Geneva, "I did not intend to adopt the religion of the city. I desired to stay there only that I might live at liberty and in security." Bruno had a pair of breeches made for himself, and the Marchese and others apparently made Bruno a gift of a sword, hat, cape and other necessities for dressing himself; in such clothing Bruno could no longer be recognized as a priest. Things apparently went well for Bruno for a time, as he entered his name in the Rector\'s Book of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Geneva" title="University of Geneva">University of Geneva</a> in May 1579. But in keeping with his personality he could not long remain silent. In August he published an attack on the work of Antoine de la Faye, a distinguished professor. He and the printer were promptly arrested. Rather than apologizing, Bruno insisted on continuing to defend his publication. He was refused the right to take <a href="/wiki/Sacrament" title="Sacrament">sacrament</a>. Though this right was eventually restored, he left Geneva.\n</p><p>\nHe went to France, arriving first in <a href="/wiki/Lyon" title="Lyon">Lyon</a>, and thereafter settling for a time (1580&#8211;1581) in <a href="/wiki/Toulouse" title="Toulouse">Toulouse</a>, where he took his doctorate in theology and was elected by students to lecture in philosophy. It seems he also attempted at this time to return to Catholicism, but was denied absolution by the Jesuit priest he approached. When religious strife broke out in the summer of 1581, he moved to Paris. There he held a cycle of thirty lectures on theological topics and also began to gain fame for his prodigious memory. Bruno\'s feats of memory were based, at least in part, on his elaborate system of mnemonics, but some of his contemporaries found it easier to attribute them to magical powers. His talents attracted the benevolent attention of the king <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_France" title="Henry III of France">Henry III</a>. The king summoned him to the court. Bruno subsequently reported </p><blockquote><p>"I got me such a name that King Henry III summoned me one day to discover from me if the memory which I possessed was natural or acquired by magic art. I satisfied him that it did not come from sorcery but from organised knowledge; and, following this, I got a book on memory printed, entitled <i>The Shadows of Ideas</i>, which I dedicated to His Majesty. Forthwith he gave me an Extraordinary Lectureship with a salary."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>In Paris Bruno enjoyed the protection of his powerful French patrons. During this period, he published several works on mnemonics, including <i><a href="/wiki/De_umbris_idearum" title="De umbris idearum">De umbris idearum</a></i> (<i>On the Shadows of Ideas</i>, 1582), <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ars_Memoriae_(book_by_Giordano_Bruno)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ars Memoriae (book by Giordano Bruno) (page does not exist)">Ars Memoriae</a></i> (<i>The Art of Memory</i>, 1582), and <i><a href="/wiki/Circe_in_the_arts#Reasoning_beasts" title="Circe in the arts">Cantus Circaeus</a></i> (<i>Circe\'s Song</i>, 1582). All of these were based on his mnemonic models of organised knowledge and experience, as opposed to the simplistic logic-based mnemonic techniques of <a href="/wiki/Petrus_Ramus" title="Petrus Ramus">Petrus Ramus</a> then becoming popular. Bruno also published a comedy summarizing some of his philosophical positions, titled <i>Il Candelaio</i> (<i>The Torchbearer</i>, 1582). In the 16th century dedications were, as a rule, approved beforehand, and hence were a way of placing a work under the protection of an individual. Given that Bruno dedicated various works to the likes of King Henry III, Sir <a href="/wiki/Philip_Sidney" title="Philip Sidney">Philip Sidney</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michel_de_Castelnau" title="Michel de Castelnau">Michel de Castelnau</a> (French Ambassador to England), and possibly <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_V" title="Pope Pius V">Pope Pius V</a>, it is apparent that this wanderer had risen sharply in status and moved in powerful circles.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="England.2C_1583.E2.80.931585"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="England,_1583&#8211;1585">England, 1583&#8211;1585</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Giordano_Bruno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: England, 1583&#8211;1585">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GiordanoBrunomnemonic.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="685" data-file-width="496" decoding="async" height="304" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/GiordanoBrunomnemonic.gif/220px-GiordanoBrunomnemonic.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/GiordanoBrunomnemonic.gif/330px-GiordanoBrunomnemonic.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/GiordanoBrunomnemonic.gif/440px-GiordanoBrunomnemonic.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GiordanoBrunomnemonic.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Woodcut illustration of one of Giordano Bruno\'s less complex mnemonic devices</div></div></div>\n<p>In April 1583, Bruno went to England with letters of recommendation from <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_France" title="Henry III of France">Henry III</a> as a guest of the French ambassador, <a href="/wiki/Michel_de_Castelnau" title="Michel de Castelnau">Michel de Castelnau</a>. There he became acquainted with the poet <a href="/wiki/Philip_Sidney" title="Philip Sidney">Philip Sidney</a> (to whom he dedicated two books) and other members of the Hermetic circle around <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Dee_(mathematician)" title="John Dee (mathematician)">John Dee</a>, though there is no evidence that Bruno ever met Dee himself. He also lectured at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxford_University" title="Oxford University">Oxford</a>, and unsuccessfully sought a teaching position there. His views were controversial, notably with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Underhill,_Bishop_of_Oxford" title="John Underhill, Bishop of Oxford">John Underhill</a>, Rector of <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_College,_Oxford" title="Lincoln College, Oxford">Lincoln College</a> and subsequently bishop of Oxford, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Abbot_(Archbishop_of_Canterbury)" title="George Abbot (Archbishop of Canterbury)">George Abbot</a>, who later became <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a>. Abbot mocked Bruno for supporting "the opinion of <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Copernicus</a> that the earth [<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>] did go round, and the heavens did stand still; whereas in truth it was his own head which rather did run round, and his brains did not stand still",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> and reports accusations that Bruno plagiarized <a href="/wiki/Marsilio_Ficino" title="Marsilio Ficino">Ficino</a>\'s work.\n</p><p>Nevertheless, his stay in England was fruitful. During that time Bruno completed and published some of his most important works, the six "Italian Dialogues," including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmological_tract" title="Cosmological tract">cosmological tracts</a> <i>La Cena de le Ceneri</i> (<i>The Ash Wednesday Supper</i>, 1584), <i>De la Causa, Principio et Uno</i> (<i>On Cause, Principle and Unity</i>, 1584), <i>De l\'Infinito, Universo e Mondi</i> (<i>On the Infinite, Universe and Worlds</i>, 1584) as well as <i>Lo Spaccio de la Bestia Trionfante</i> (<i>The Expulsion of the Triumphant Beast</i>, 1584) and <i>De gl\' Heroici Furori</i> (<i>On the Heroic Frenzies</i>, 1585). Some of these were printed by <a href="/wiki/John_Charlewood" title="John Charlewood">John Charlewood</a>. Some of the works that Bruno published in London, notably <i>The Ash Wednesday Supper</i>, appear to have given offense. Once again, Bruno\'s controversial views and tactless language lost him the support of his friends. John Bossy has advanced the theory that, while staying in the French Embassy in London, Bruno was also spying on Catholic conspirators, under the pseudonym "Henry Fagot", for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Francis_Walsingham" title="Sir Francis Walsingham">Sir Francis Walsingham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Queen Elizabeth</a>\'s Secretary of State.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bruno is sometimes cited as being the first to propose that the universe is infinite, which he did during his time in England, but an English scientist, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Digges" title="Thomas Digges">Thomas Digges</a>, put forth this idea in a published work in 1576, some eight years earlier than Bruno.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Last_years_of_wandering.2C_1585.E2.80.931592"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_years_of_wandering,_1585&#8211;1592">Last years of wandering, 1585&#8211;1592</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Giordano_Bruno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Last years of wandering, 1585&#8211;1592">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In October 1585, after the French embassy in London was attacked by a mob, Bruno returned to Paris with Castelnau, finding a tense political situation. Moreover, his 120 theses against <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotelian</a> natural science and his pamphlets against the mathematician <a href="/wiki/Fabrizio_Mordente" title="Fabrizio Mordente">Fabrizio Mordente</a> soon put him in ill favor. In 1586, following a violent quarrel about Mordente\'s invention, the differential compass, he left France for (Germany).\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Unamed_Figures.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1464" data-file-width="1476" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4f/Unamed_Figures.jpg/220px-Unamed_Figures.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4f/Unamed_Figures.jpg/330px-Unamed_Figures.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4f/Unamed_Figures.jpg/440px-Unamed_Figures.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Unamed_Figures.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Woodcut from "Articuli centum et sexaginta adversus huius tempestatis mathematicos atque philosophos," <a href="/wiki/Prague" title="Prague">Prague</a> 1588</div></div></div><p>In Germany he failed to obtain a teaching position at <a href="/wiki/Marburg" title="Marburg">Marburg</a>, but was granted permission to teach at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Halle-Wittenberg" title="University of Halle-Wittenberg">Wittenberg</a>, where he lectured on <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> for two years. However, with a change of intellectual climate there, he was no longer welcome, and went in 1588 to <a href="/wiki/Prague" title="Prague">Prague</a>, where he obtained 300 <a href="/wiki/Thaler" title="Thaler">taler</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rudolf_II" title="Rudolf II">Rudolf II</a>, but no teaching position. He went on to serve briefly as a professor in <a href="/wiki/University_of_Helmstedt" title="University of Helmstedt">Helmstedt</a>, but had to flee again when he was <a href="/wiki/Excommunication" title="Excommunication">excommunicated</a> by the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lutherianism&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lutherianism (page does not exist)">Lutherans</a>.\n</p><p>During this period he produced several <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> works, dictated to his friend and secretary Girolamo Besler, including <i>De Magia</i> (<i>On Magic</i>), <i>Theses De Magia</i> (<i>Theses on Magic</i>) and <i>De Vinculis in Genere</i> (<i>A General Account of Bonding</i>). All these were apparently transcribed or recorded by Besler (or Bisler) between 1589 and 1590.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> He also published <i>De Imaginum, Signorum, Et Idearum Compositione</i> (<i>On the Composition of Images, Signs and Ideas</i>, 1591).\n</p><p>In 1591 he was in <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">Frankfurt</a>. Apparently, during the <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt_Book_Fair" title="Frankfurt Book Fair">Frankfurt Book Fair</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> he received an invitation to Venice from the patrician Giovanni <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mocenigo_family" title="Mocenigo family">Mocenigo</a>, who wished to be instructed in the art of memory, and also heard of a vacant chair in mathematics at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Padua" title="University of Padua">University of Padua</a>. At the time the <a href="/wiki/Inquisition" title="Inquisition">Inquisition</a> seemed to be losing some of its strictness, and because Venice was the most liberal state in Italy, Bruno was lulled into making the fatal mistake of returning to Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p><p>He went first to <a href="/wiki/Padua" title="Padua">Padua</a>, where he taught briefly, and applied unsuccessfully for the chair of mathematics, which was given instead to <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> one year later. Bruno accepted Mocenigo\'s invitation and moved to Venice in March 1592. For about two months he served as an in-house tutor to Mocenigo. When Bruno announced his plan to leave Venice to his host, the latter, who was unhappy with the teachings he had received and had apparently come to dislike Bruno, denounced him to the Venetian Inquisition, which had Bruno arrested on 22 May 1592. Among the numerous charges of <a href="/wiki/Blasphemy" title="Blasphemy">blasphemy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heresy</a> brought against him in Venice, based on Mocenigo\'s denunciation, was his belief in the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_pluralism" title="Cosmic pluralism">plurality of worlds</a>, as well as accusations of personal misconduct. Bruno defended himself skillfully, stressing the philosophical character of some of his positions, denying others and admitting that he had had doubts on some matters of dogma. The Roman Inquisition, however, asked for his transfer to Rome. After several months of argument, the Venetian authorities reluctantly consented and Bruno was sent to Rome in February 1593.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Imprisonment.2C_trial_and_execution.2C_1593.E2.80.931600"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Imprisonment,_trial_and_execution,_1593&#8211;1600">Imprisonment, trial and execution, 1593&#8211;1600</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Giordano_Bruno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Imprisonment, trial and execution, 1593&#8211;1600">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>During the seven years of his trial in Rome, Bruno was held in confinement, lastly in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tower_of_Nona" title="Tower of Nona">Tower of Nona</a>. Some important documents about the trial are lost, but others have been preserved, among them a summary of the proceedings that was rediscovered in 1940.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The numerous charges against Bruno, based on some of his books as well as on witness accounts, included blasphemy, immoral conduct, and heresy in matters of dogmatic theology, and involved some of the basic doctrines of his philosophy and cosmology. <a href="/wiki/Luigi_Firpo" title="Luigi Firpo">Luigi Firpo</a> speculates the charges made against Bruno by the Roman Inquisition were:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>holding opinions contrary to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholicism" title="Catholicism">Catholic faith</a> and speaking against it and its ministers;</li>\n<li>holding opinions contrary to the Catholic faith about the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a>, divinity of Christ, and <a href="/wiki/Incarnation_(Christianity)" title="Incarnation (Christianity)">Incarnation</a>;</li>\n<li>holding opinions contrary to the Catholic faith pertaining to Jesus as Christ;</li>\n<li>holding opinions contrary to the Catholic faith regarding the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mary_(mother_of_Jesus)" title="Mary (mother of Jesus)">virginity of Mary, mother of Jesus</a>;</li>\n<li>holding opinions contrary to the Catholic faith about both <a href="/wiki/Transubstantiation" title="Transubstantiation">Transubstantiation</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_(Catholic_Church)" title="Mass (Catholic Church)">Mass</a>;</li>\n<li>claiming the existence of a <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_pluralism" title="Cosmic pluralism">plurality of worlds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eternity_of_the_world" title="Eternity of the world">their eternity</a>;</li>\n<li>believing in <a href="/wiki/Metempsychosis" title="Metempsychosis">metempsychosis</a> and in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transmigration_of_the_soul" title="Transmigration of the soul">transmigration</a> of the human soul into brutes;</li>\n<li>dealing in magics and divination.</li></ul>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Relief_Bruno_Campo_dei_Fiori_n1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1290" data-file-width="2080" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Relief_Bruno_Campo_dei_Fiori_n1.jpg/330px-Relief_Bruno_Campo_dei_Fiori_n1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Relief_Bruno_Campo_dei_Fiori_n1.jpg/495px-Relief_Bruno_Campo_dei_Fiori_n1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Relief_Bruno_Campo_dei_Fiori_n1.jpg/660px-Relief_Bruno_Campo_dei_Fiori_n1.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Relief_Bruno_Campo_dei_Fiori_n1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The trial of Giordano Bruno by the Roman Inquisition. Bronze relief by Ettore Ferrari, <a href="/wiki/Campo_de%27_Fiori" title="Campo de\' Fiori">Campo de\' Fiori</a>, Rome.</div></div></div>\n<p>Bruno defended himself as he had in Venice, insisting that he accepted the Church\'s dogmatic teachings, but trying to preserve the basis of his philosophy. In particular, he held firm to his belief in the plurality of worlds, although he was admonished to abandon it. His trial was overseen by the Inquisitor Cardinal <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bellarmine" title="Robert Bellarmine">Bellarmine</a>, who demanded a full recantation, which Bruno eventually refused. On 20 January 1600, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Clement_VIII" title="Pope Clement VIII">Pope Clement VIII</a> declared Bruno a heretic and the Inquisition issued a sentence of death. According to the correspondence of <a href="/wiki/Caspar_Schoppe" title="Caspar Schoppe">Gaspar Schopp</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breslau" title="Breslau">Breslau</a>, he is said to have made a threatening gesture towards his judges and to have replied: <i>Maiori forsan cum timore sententiam in me fertis quam ego accipiam</i> ("Perhaps you pronounce this sentence against me with greater fear than I receive it").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p><p>He was turned over to the secular authorities. On Ash Wednesday, 17 February 1600, in the <a href="/wiki/Campo_de%27_Fiori" title="Campo de\' Fiori">Campo de\' Fiori</a> (a central Roman market square), with his "tongue imprisoned because of his wicked words". He was hung upside down naked before he was finally <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burned_at_the_stake" title="Burned at the stake">burned at the stake</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fitzgerald2007_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fitzgerald2007-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> His ashes were thrown into the <a href="/wiki/Tiber" title="Tiber">Tiber</a> river. All of Bruno\'s works were placed on the <i><a href="/wiki/Index_Librorum_Prohibitorum" title="Index Librorum Prohibitorum">Index Librorum Prohibitorum</a></i> in 1603.\nThe inquisition cardinals who judged Giordano Bruno were <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bellarmine" title="Robert Bellarmine">Cardinal Bellarmino (Bellarmine)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carlo_Gaudenzio_Madruzzo" title="Carlo Gaudenzio Madruzzo">Cardinal Madruzzo (Madruzzi)</a>, Cardinal Camillo Borghese (later <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_V" title="Pope Paul V">Pope Paul V</a>), Domenico Cardinal Pinelli, Pompeio Cardinal Arrigoni, <a href="/wiki/Paolo_Emilio_Sfondrati" title="Paolo Emilio Sfondrati">Cardinal Sfondrati</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pedro_de_Deza" title="Pedro de Deza">Pedro Cardinal De Deza Manuel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Giulio_Antonio_Santorio" title="Giulio Antonio Santorio">Cardinal Santorio</a> (Archbishop of Santa Severina, Cardinal-Bishop of Palestrina).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_appearance">Physical appearance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Giordano_Bruno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Physical appearance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The earliest likeness of Bruno is an engraving published in 1715<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> and cited by Salvestrini as "the only known portrait of Bruno". Salvestrini suggests that it is a re-engraving made from a now lost original.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-22">[22]</a></sup> This engraving has provided the source for later images.\n</p><p>The records of Bruno\'s imprisonment by the Venetian inquisition in May 1592 describe him as a man "of average height, with a hazel-coloured beard and the appearance of being about forty years of age".\nAlternately, a passage in a work by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Abbot_(Archbishop_of_Canterbury)" title="George Abbot (Archbishop of Canterbury)">George Abbot</a> indicates that Bruno was of diminutive stature: "When that Italian Didapper, who intituled himselfe Philotheus Iordanus Brunus Nolanus, magis elaboratae Theologiae Doctor, &amp;c. with a name longer than his body...".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The word "didapper" used by Abbot is the derisive term which at the time meant "a small diving waterfowl".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmology">Cosmology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Giordano_Bruno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Cosmology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_cosmological_beliefs">Contemporary cosmological beliefs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Giordano_Bruno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Contemporary cosmological beliefs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Celestial_spheres#History" title="Celestial spheres">Celestial spheres &#167;&#160;History</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bartolomeu_Velho_1568.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2402" data-file-width="3255" decoding="async" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Bartolomeu_Velho_1568.jpg/330px-Bartolomeu_Velho_1568.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Bartolomeu_Velho_1568.jpg/495px-Bartolomeu_Velho_1568.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Bartolomeu_Velho_1568.jpg/660px-Bartolomeu_Velho_1568.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bartolomeu_Velho_1568.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illuminated illustration of the Ptolemaic geocentric conception of the universe. The outermost text reads "The heavenly empire, dwelling of God and all the selected"</div></div></div>\n<p>In the first half of the 15th century, <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_of_Cusa" title="Nicholas of Cusa">Nicholas of Cusa</a> challenged the then widely accepted philosophies of <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelianism</a>, envisioning instead an infinite universe whose center was everywhere and circumference nowhere, and moreover teeming with countless stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> He also predicted that neither were the rotational orbits circular nor were their movements uniform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the second half of the 16th century, the theories of Copernicus (1473&#8211;1543) began diffusing through Europe. Copernicus conserved the idea of planets fixed to solid spheres, but considered the apparent motion of the stars to be an illusion caused by the rotation of the Earth on its axis; he also preserved the notion of an immobile center, but it was the Sun rather than the Earth. Copernicus also argued the Earth was a planet orbiting the Sun once every year. However he maintained the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_model" title="Ptolemaic model">Ptolemaic hypothesis</a> that the orbits of the planets were composed of perfect circles&#8212;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deferents" title="Deferents">deferents</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epicycles" title="Epicycles">epicycles</a>&#8212;and that the stars were fixed on a stationary outer sphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Despite the widespread publication of Copernicus\' work <i><a href="/wiki/De_revolutionibus_orbium_coelestium" title="De revolutionibus orbium coelestium">De revolutionibus orbium coelestium</a></i>, during Bruno\'s time most educated Catholics subscribed to the Aristotelian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geocentrism" title="Geocentrism">geocentric</a> view that the Earth was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_Center_of_the_Universe" title="History of the Center of the Universe">center of the universe</a>, and that all heavenly bodies revolved around it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The ultimate limit of the universe was the <i>primum mobile</i>, whose diurnal rotation was conferred upon it by a <a href="/wiki/Transcendence_(religion)" title="Transcendence (religion)">transcendental</a> God, not part of the universe (although, as the <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Empyrean_(disambiguation)" title="Empyrean (disambiguation)">kingdom of heaven</a>, <a href="/wiki/Celestial_spheres" title="Celestial spheres">adjacent to it</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>), a motionless <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_argument" title="Cosmological argument">prime mover</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_cause" title="First cause">first cause</a>. The fixed stars were part of this celestial sphere, all at the same fixed distance from the immobile Earth at the center of the sphere. <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> had numbered these at 1,022, grouped into 48 <a href="/wiki/Constellation" title="Constellation">constellations</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a> were each fixed to a transparent sphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Few <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomers</a> of Bruno\'s time accepted <a href="/wiki/Copernican_heliocentrism" title="Copernican heliocentrism">Copernicus\'s heliocentric model</a>. Among those who did were the Germans <a href="/wiki/Michael_Maestlin" title="Michael Maestlin">Michael Maestlin</a> (1550&#8211;1631), <a href="/wiki/Christoph_Rothmann" title="Christoph Rothmann">Christoph Rothmann</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a> (1571&#8211;1630), the Englishman <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Digges" title="Thomas Digges">Thomas Digges</a>, author of <i>A Perfit Description of the Caelestial Orbes,</i> and the Italian <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> (1564&#8211;1642).\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Bruno.27s_cosmological_claims"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Bruno\'s_cosmological_claims">Bruno\'s cosmological claims</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Giordano_Bruno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Bruno\'s cosmological claims">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1584, Bruno published two important philosophical dialogues (La Cena de le Ceneri and De l\'infinito universo et mondi) in which he argued against the planetary spheres (Christoph Rothmann did the same in 1586 as did <a href="/wiki/Tycho_Brahe" title="Tycho Brahe">Tycho Brahe</a> in 1587) and affirmed the Copernican principle.\n</p><p>In particular, to support the Copernican view and oppose the objection according to which the motion of the Earth would be perceived by means of the motion of winds, clouds etc., in La Cena de le Ceneri Bruno anticipates some of the arguments of Galilei on the relativity principle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Note that he also uses the example now known as <a href="/wiki/Galileo%27s_ship" title="Galileo\'s ship">Galileo\'s ship</a>.\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>Theophilus  &#8211; [...] air through which the clouds and winds move are parts of the Earth, [...] to mean under the name of Earth the whole machinery and the entire animated part, which consists of dissimilar parts; so that the rivers, the rocks, the seas, the whole vaporous and turbulent air, which is enclosed within the highest mountains, should belong to the Earth as its members, just as the air [does] in the lungs and in other cavities of animals by which they breathe, widen their arteries, and other similar effects necessary for life are performed. The clouds, too, move through accidents in the body of the Earth and are in its bowels as are the waters. [...]\nWith the Earth move [...] all things that are on the Earth.  If, therefore, from a point outside the Earth something were thrown upon the Earth, it would lose, because of the latter\'s motion, its straightness as would be seen on the ship [...] moving along a river, if someone on point C of the riverbank were to throw a stone along a straight line, and would see the stone miss its target by the amount of the velocity of the ship\'s motion.  But if someone were placed high on the mast of that ship, move as it may however fast, he would not miss his target at all, so that the stone or some other heavy thing thrown downward would not come along a straight line from the point E which is at the top of the mast, or cage, to the point D which is at the bottom of the mast, or at some point in the bowels and body of the ship.  Thus, if from the point D to the point E someone who is inside the ship would throw a stone straight up, it would return to the bottom along the same line however far the ship moved, provided it was not subject to any pitch and roll."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>Bruno\'s infinite universe was filled with a substance&#8212;a "pure air," <a href="/wiki/Aether_(classical_element)" title="Aether (classical element)">aether</a>, or <i>spiritus</i>&#8212;that offered no resistance to the heavenly bodies which, in Bruno\'s view, rather than being fixed, moved under their own <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Impetus_(mechanics)" title="Impetus (mechanics)">impetus</a> (momentum). Most dramatically, he completely abandoned the idea of a <a href="/wiki/Hierarchy" title="Hierarchy">hierarchical</a> universe.\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>The universe is then one, infinite, immobile.... It is not capable of comprehension and therefore is endless and limitless, and to that extent infinite and indeterminable, and consequently immobile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>Bruno\'s cosmology distinguishes between "suns" which produce their own light and heat, and have other bodies moving around them; and "earths" which move around suns and receive light and heat from them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thirddialogue_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-thirddialogue-48">[48]</a></sup> Bruno suggested that some, if not all, of the objects classically known as <a href="/wiki/Fixed_stars" title="Fixed stars">fixed stars</a> are in fact suns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thirddialogue_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-thirddialogue-48">[48]</a></sup> According to astrophysicist <a href="/wiki/Steven_Soter" title="Steven Soter">Steven Soter</a>, he was the first person to grasp that "stars are other suns with their own planets."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bruno wrote that other worlds "have no less virtue nor a nature different from that of our Earth" and, like Earth, "contain animals and inhabitants".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the late 16th century, and throughout the 17th century, Bruno\'s ideas were held up for ridicule, debate, or inspiration. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Margaret_Cavendish" title="Margaret Cavendish">Margaret Cavendish</a>, for example, wrote an entire series of poems against "atoms" and "infinite worlds" in <i>Poems and Fancies</i> in 1664. Bruno\'s true, if partial, vindication would have to wait for the implications and impact of <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Newtonian</a> cosmology.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>Bruno\'s overall contribution to the birth of modern science is still controversial. Some scholars follow Frances Yates stressing the importance of Bruno\'s ideas about the universe being infinite and lacking geocentric structure as a crucial crosspoint between the old and the new. Others see in Bruno\'s idea of multiple worlds instantiating the infinite possibilities of a pristine, indivisible One,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> a forerunner of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hugh_Everett" title="Hugh Everett">Everett</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Many-worlds_interpretation" title="Many-worlds interpretation">many-worlds interpretation</a> of quantum mechanics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While most academics note Bruno\'s theological position as <a href="/wiki/Pantheism" title="Pantheism">pantheism</a>, physicist and philosopher <a href="/wiki/Max_Bernhard_Weinstein" title="Max Bernhard Weinstein">Max Bernhard Weinstein</a> in his <i>Welt- und Lebensanschauungen, Hervorgegangen aus Religion, Philosophie und Naturerkenntnis</i> ("World and Life Views, Emerging From Religion, Philosophy and Nature"), wrote that the theological model of <a href="/wiki/Pandeism" title="Pandeism">pandeism</a> was strongly expressed in the teachings of Bruno, especially with respect to the vision of a deity which had no particular relation to one part of the infinite universe more than any other, and was <a href="/wiki/Immanence" title="Immanence">immanent</a>, as present on Earth as in the Heavens, subsuming in itself the multiplicity of existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Retrospective_views_of_Bruno">Retrospective views of Bruno</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Giordano_Bruno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Retrospective views of Bruno">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Monument_to_Giordano_Bruno_in_Campo_de%27_Fiori_square_-_Rome,_Italy_-_6_June_2014.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Monument_to_Giordano_Bruno_in_Campo_de%27_Fiori_square_-_Rome%2C_Italy_-_6_June_2014.jpg/440px-Monument_to_Giordano_Bruno_in_Campo_de%27_Fiori_square_-_Rome%2C_Italy_-_6_June_2014.jpg class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Monument_to_Giordano_Bruno_at_Potsdamer_Platz_in_Berlin._Germany.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2725" data-file-width="3218" decoding="async" height="186" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Monument_to_Giordano_Bruno_at_Potsdamer_Platz_in_Berlin._Germany.jpg/330px-Monument_to_Giordano_Bruno_at_Potsdamer_Platz_in_Berlin._Germany.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Monument_to_Giordano_Bruno_at_Potsdamer_Platz_in_Berlin._Germany.jpg/440px-Monument_to_Giordano_Bruno_at_Potsdamer_Platz_in_Berlin._Germany.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Monument_to_Giordano_Bruno_at_Potsdamer_Platz_in_Berlin._Germany.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Monument to Giordano Bruno at Potsdamer Platz in Berlin, Germany, referencing his burning at the stake while tied upside down.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_Vatican_position">Late Vatican position</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Giordano_Bruno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Late Vatican position">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Vatican has published few official statements about Bruno\'s trial and execution. In 1942, Cardinal <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Mercati" title="Giovanni Mercati">Giovanni Mercati</a>, who discovered a number of lost documents relating to Bruno\'s trial, stated that the Church was perfectly justified in condemning him. On the 400th anniversary of Bruno\'s death, in 2000, Cardinal <a href="/wiki/Angelo_Sodano" title="Angelo Sodano">Angelo Sodano</a> declared Bruno\'s death to be a "sad episode" but, despite his regret, he defended Bruno\'s prosecutors, maintaining that the Inquisitors "had the desire to serve freedom and promote the common good and did everything possible to save his life".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> In the same year, Pope <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Paul_II" title="John Paul II">John Paul II</a> made a general apology for "the use of violence that some have committed in the service of truth".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="A_martyr_of_science">A martyr of science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Giordano_Bruno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: A martyr of science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Some authors have characterized Bruno as a "martyr of science," suggesting parallels with the <a href="/wiki/Galileo_affair" title="Galileo affair">Galileo affair</a> which began around 1610.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> They assert that, even though Bruno\'s theological beliefs, or perceptions of them by others, were an important factor in his heresy trial, his <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus#Copernicanism" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Copernicanism</a> and cosmological beliefs played a significant role in the outcome.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>"It should not be supposed", writes A. M. Paterson of Bruno and his "heliocentric solar system," that he "reached his conclusions via some mystical revelation....His work is an essential part of the scientific and philosophical developments that he initiated."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Paterson echoes Hegel in writing that Bruno "ushers in a modern theory of knowledge that understands all natural things in the universe to be known by the human mind through the mind\'s dialectical structure".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ingegno writes that Bruno embraced the philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Lucretius" title="Lucretius">Lucretius</a>, "aimed at liberating man from the fear of death and the gods."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Characters in Bruno\'s <i>Cause, Principle and Unity</i> desire "to improve speculative science and knowledge of natural things," and to achieve a philosophy "which brings about the perfection of the human intellect most easily and eminently, and most closely corresponds to the truth of nature."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Other scholars oppose such views, and claim Bruno\'s martyrdom to science to be exaggerated, or outright false. For Yates, while "nineteenth century liberals" were thrown "into ecstasies" over Bruno\'s Copernicanism, "Bruno pushes Copernicus\' scientific work back into a prescientific stage, back into Hermetism, interpreting the Copernican diagram as a hieroglyph of divine mysteries."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to historian Mordechai Feingold, "Both admirers and critics of Giordano Bruno basically agree that he was pompous and arrogant, highly valuing his opinions and showing little patience with anyone who even mildly disagreed with him." Discussing Bruno\'s experience of rejection when he visited Oxford University, Feingold suggests that "it might have been Bruno\'s manner, his language and his self-assertiveness, rather than his ideas" that caused offence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeingoldVickers1984_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FeingoldVickers1984-62">[62]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theological_heresy">Theological heresy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Giordano_Bruno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Theological heresy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In his <i><a href="/wiki/Lectures_on_the_History_of_Philosophy" title="Lectures on the History of Philosophy">Lectures on the History of Philosophy</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel" title="Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel">Hegel</a> writes that Bruno\'s life represented "a bold rejection of all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic" title="Catholic">Catholic</a> beliefs resting on mere authority."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Alfonso Ingegno states that Bruno\'s philosophy "challenges the developments of the Reformation, calls into question the truth-value of the whole of Christianity, and claims that Christ perpetrated a deceit on mankind... Bruno suggests that we can now recognize the universal law which controls the perpetual becoming of all things in an infinite universe."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-causa_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-causa-64">[64]</a></sup> A. M. Paterson says that, while we no longer have a copy of the official papal condemnation of Bruno, his heresies included "the doctrine of the infinite universe and the innumerable worlds" and his beliefs "on the movement of the earth".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Michael White notes that the Inquisition may have pursued Bruno early in his life on the basis of his opposition to <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, interest in <a href="/wiki/Arianism" title="Arianism">Arianism</a>, reading of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Desiderius_Erasmus" title="Desiderius Erasmus">Erasmus</a>, and possession of banned texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-white_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-white-66">[66]</a></sup> White considers that Bruno\'s later heresy was "multifaceted" and may have rested on his conception of infinite worlds. "This was perhaps the most dangerous notion of all... If other worlds existed with intelligent beings living there, did they too have their visitations? The idea was quite unthinkable."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-white_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-white-66">[66]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Frances Yates rejects what she describes as the "legend that Bruno was prosecuted as a philosophical thinker, was burned for his daring views on innumerable worlds or on the movement of the earth." Yates however writes that "the Church was... perfectly within its rights if it included philosophical points in its condemnation of Bruno\'s heresies" because "the philosophical points were quite inseparable from the heresies."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Stanford_Encyclopedia_of_Philosophy" title="Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy">Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy</a></i>, "in 1600 there was no official Catholic position on the Copernican system, and it was certainly not a heresy. When [...] Bruno [...] was burned at the stake as a heretic, it had nothing to do with his writings in support of Copernican cosmology."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nThe website of the <a href="/wiki/Vatican_Secret_Archives" title="Vatican Secret Archives">Vatican Secret Archives</a>, discussing a summary of legal proceedings against Bruno in Rome, states: </p><blockquote><p>"In the same rooms where Giordano Bruno was questioned, for the same important reasons of the relationship between science and faith, at the dawning of the new astronomy and at the decline of Aristotle\'s philosophy, sixteen years later, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bellarmine" title="Robert Bellarmine">Cardinal Bellarmino</a>, who then contested Bruno\'s heretical theses, summoned Galileo Galilei, who also faced a famous inquisitorial trial, which, luckily for him, ended with a simple abjuration."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Artistic_depictions">Artistic depictions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Giordano_Bruno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Artistic depictions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Following the 1870 <a href="/wiki/Capture_of_Rome" title="Capture of Rome">Capture of Rome</a> by the newly created <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy_(1861%E2%80%931946)" title="Kingdom of Italy (1861&#8211;1946)">Kingdom of Italy</a> and the end of the Church\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temporal_power_(Papal)" title="Temporal power (Papal)">temporal power</a> over the city, the erection of a <a href="/wiki/Statue_of_Giordano_Bruno" title="Statue of Giordano Bruno">monument to Bruno</a> on the site of his execution became feasible. The monument was sharply opposed by the clerical party, but was finally erected by the Rome Municipality and inaugurated in 1889.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A statue of a stretched human figure standing on its head, designed by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Polzin" title="Alexander Polzin">Alexander Polzin</a> and depicting Bruno\'s death at the stake, was placed in <a href="/wiki/Potsdamer_Platz" title="Potsdamer Platz">Potsdamer Platz</a> station in <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a> on 2 March 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>\n<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Retrospective iconography of Bruno shows him with a Dominican <a href="/wiki/Cowl" title="Cowl">cowl</a> but not <a href="/wiki/Tonsure" title="Tonsure">tonsured</a>. Edward Gosselin has suggested that it is likely Bruno kept his tonsure at least until 1579, and it is possible that he wore it again thereafter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>\n</p><p>An idealized animated version of Bruno appears in the first episode of the 2014 television series <i><a href="/wiki/Cosmos:_A_Spacetime_Odyssey" title="Cosmos: A Spacetime Odyssey">Cosmos: A Spacetime Odyssey</a></i>. In this depiction, Bruno is shown with a more modern look, without tonsure and wearing clerical robes and without his hood. <i>Cosmos</i> presents Bruno as an impoverished philosopher who was ultimately executed due to his refusal to recant his belief in other worlds, a portrayal that was criticized by some as simplistic or historically inaccurate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncse_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-ncse-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 2016 song "Roman Sky" by hard rock band <a href="/wiki/Avenged_Sevenfold" title="Avenged Sevenfold">Avenged Sevenfold</a> focuses on the death of Bruno.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Also the song <i>"Anima Mundi"</i> by Massimiliano Larocca and the album <i>Numen Lumen</i> by <a href="/wiki/Neofolk" title="Neofolk">neofolk</a> group <a href="/wiki/Hautville" title="Hautville">Hautville</a>, which tracks Bruno\'s lyrics, were dedicated to the philosopher.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References_in_poetry">References in poetry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Giordano_Bruno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: References in poetry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Algernon_Charles_Swinburne" title="Algernon Charles Swinburne">Algernon Charles Swinburne</a> wrote a poem honouring Giordano Bruno in 1889, when the statue of Bruno was constructed in Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Czeslaw_Milosz" title="Czeslaw Milosz">Czeslaw Milosz</a> evokes the story and image of Giordano Bruno in his poem "Campo Dei Fiori" (Warsaw 1943).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Heather_McHugh" title="Heather McHugh">Heather McHugh</a> depicted Bruno as the principal of a story told (at dinner, by an "underestimated" travel guide) to a group of contemporary American poets in Rome.  The poem (originally published in McHugh\'s collection of poems <i>Hinge &amp; Sign</i>, nominee for the <a href="/wiki/National_Book_Award" title="National Book Award">National Book Award</a>, and subsequently reprinted widely) channels the very question of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ars_poetica" title="Ars poetica">ars poetica</a>, or meta-meaning itself, through the embedded narrative of the suppression of Bruno\'s words, silenced towards the end of his life both literally and literarily.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Appearances_in_fiction">Appearances in fiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Giordano_Bruno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Appearances in fiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="box-More_citations_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png"
